==PAGE:3==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Preface
The following report is prepared as a Master Thesis in order to achieve a ﬁnal Master Degree at
Technical University of Denmark, Department of Civil Engineering.
The scope of the project is how to calculate and estimate the heeded TBM face support pressure in
order to predict the face pressure of a future TBM excavation.
The title of this thesis is "Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels"
and the thesis will count for 30 ECTS-points.
The project contains the following:
1 Presentation of the basic principles of Earth Pressure Balanced Tunnel Boring Machines.
2 Presentation of four methods of calculating the needed face support pressure.
3 Analysis of two preformed TBM excavations and one future TBM excavation.
The following programs has been used in order to prepare this report:
• LaTeX.
• Maple 18.
• MS Excel.
• MS Paint
• PLAXIS 2D.
• PLAXIS 3D.
• Origin 9.
The thesis is handed in as a pdf-ﬁle uploaded to the DTU Campus-net. Two hard copies are handed
in as well including a USB-pen containing the generated data ﬁles of the performed FEM-analysis
All data and information utilized in this project is kindly provided by the Metroselskabet I/S
A special note of gratitude should be sent to Søren Degn Eskesen of Metoselskabet/COWI for
suggesting the subject of this thesis. To Neil Martini of Metroselskabet/Arup and Ivi Tatiana Arce
Juliao of Metoselskabet/COWI for technical and theoretical support. Finally, it should be noted
that Katrine Alling Andreasen has worked as supervisor to this project.
ii
==PAGE:4==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Summary
The scope of this Master Thesis is to examine the inﬂuence of the applied TBM face support
pressure in relation to settlement control and management, especially in the break-in situation.
By investigating and discussing four diﬀerent approaches of calculation the required face support
pressure and by comparing these calculations to real life situations a number of conclusions are made.
Two diﬀerent already performed TBM break-in cases have been investigated. The case of the Sønder
Boulevard break-in and the case of the Frederiksberg Station break-in. Both cases are part of the
Copenhagen Metro Cityringen project.
The two cases have been examined using this procedure:
1 Analytical Calculations of Settlements
2 2D Numerical Calculations of Settlements
3 Calculation of Face Pressure
4 3D Numerical Calculations of Settlements
5 Presentations of Recorded Settlements
6 Comparison
From the knowledge gained by these two case studies, an attempt to predict the soil behaviour and
to suggest the needed percussions of a third and future break-in case was made. This third case
is the case of Rådhuspladsen Station which is situated in a particular challenging soil formation
containing a large bulk of assumable unstable and granular soil.

Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
1
Introduction
The cities of today’s world are a closed network of transportation systems, utilities and residential
and industrial buildings[1]. Worldwide, millions of people live and work in such major cities. Very
often in restricted and congested spaces and the number of people occupying the cities are expected
to grow signiﬁcantly in number within the nearest future. The expected growth in population will
transform many cities of today from small to medium, medium to large and from large to mega
cities.
Figure 1.1: Breakthrough of the Herrenknecht TBM at Sao Bento Station, Porto, Portugal [1].
1
==PAGE:14==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
The transformation will result in a constant demand for a proper allocation and redistribution of
the limited urban space to the various urban functions, existing ones as well as future ones. In
order to meet this demand city planners, politician, architects and engineers have turned to an
obvious source of available space, namely the underground space in an increasingly extend.
In fact, since the beginning of the industrialised era underground spaces have historically been
created in urban areas and mainly used to host traﬃc ways, such as streets, subways, railways etc.
and public-service utilities such as water supply ducts and sewers. Nowadays, underground space is
created for several other purposes than the above mentioned. Reasons such as underground car
parking, power supply and waste water management
The ever-increasing need for tunnels in urban areas is one of the most eﬃcient prime factors
of the development of tunnelling technologies and, in particular, for mechanized excavation. In the
later case, the continuous search for fast and safe solutions in any soil environment and underground
conditions has signiﬁcantly increased the feasibility limits for the accomplishment of tunnelling.
Today it is possible to excavate tunnels rapidly under small overburden, in loose ground and under
a water table. At the same time it is possible to minimize the ground settlements and reducing the
disturbance to the surface activities in the urbanized area. All this is due to the great developments
in mechanized excavation technologies achieved in the last 30 years.
1.1
Advantages of mechanized tunnelling.
Compared with the conventional tunnel excavation methods the increase in the use of mechanized
techniques in urban tunnelling is mostly due to the following advantages [1]:
• The work environment is "factory" like and characterized by higher levels of comfort and
safety for the workers compared to a "mining" like working environment.
• The rapidity and industrialisation of the construction cycle with possible automation for all
the construction processes and activities will, consequently, shorten the construction time.
• The possibility to measure and control the principal construction parameters like the quantity
of excavated material, the support pressure applied the excavation face, the ground movements
around the tunnel periphery and the surface settlements.
• The low noise levels, limited dust dispersion in the environment and the minimum disturbance
to the water table.
• The use of pre-cast segments for the tunnel lining facilitates the control of the construction
phases and enhance the quality of the ﬁnished work.
• The overall cost is often lower than the conventional tunnelling methods particular in regard
to longer tunnel stretches.
In addition to the above it should be stated that in many cases, the desired tunnel, such as the
crossing of a railway under important historical sites, could not have been build in urban areas,
except through mechanized excavation due to better control of the large number of risks involved
in the build.
1.2
Particular challenges of urban tunnelling.
Several challenges meet the owners, the planner, the designers and the constructors when the
development of infrastructure in the related underground space is required. In comparison with
tunnelling in open space rural areas, tunnelling in urban areas has some major and speciﬁc
characteristics and constrains as listed below [1].
• The layout is strictly related to the ﬁnal use of the functional aspect of the tunnel. However,
many constrains intervene in order to limit the alignment location, resulting in frequent and
2
==PAGE:15==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
often unavoidable, potential interference with buildings at the surface, underground utilities
and other existing underground structures.
• The accessibility for carrying out the necessary site investigations can be limited due to a
lack of permission or to the occupation of the surface.
• Urban tunnelling is generally carried out at a shallow depth for functional and cost reasons.
This gives rise to a series of consequences in terms of geology, sub-surface and impacts.
• The sub-surface at shallow depth often consist of loose soils, alluvial deposits or man-made
ﬁlls. The poor quality of the ground is one of the key factors for the tunnel design and
construction.
• The immediate underground level at the sub-surface is reserved to the installation of un-
derground utilities that have to be identiﬁed and assessed, in terms of the risk of potential
damage caused by tunnelling-induced settlements and subsequently diverted or relocated
permanently, if needed.
• In many parts of the world, the cities have an important historical background. Hence, in the
immediate underground level of the sub-surface, important archaeological features could be
hidden. These features have to be recognized and dealt with, especially when planning the
tunnel accesses or the service shafts.
• Urban tunnelling at shallow depth usually induces settlements at the surface, even under
the most strictly controlled operation. The magnitude of these settlements is a function of
many interrelated factors such as the quality of the ground, the behaviour of the ground when
tunnelling, the control of the tunnel face and tunnel section stability during construction, the
presence of underground water and the hydrogeological regime. etc.
• The high level of interaction with life above the surface has to be analysed and solved carefully
applying solutions that can be accepted by the public without causing major disturbance.
This requires an appropriate plan for the temporary diversion of the traﬃc, an accurate
planning of work-site areas, particular attention to control of dust and noise emissions and a
special care for safety issues.
In general, urban tunnelling is often related to the implementation of strategic infrastructure
projects, which have high politic relevance and public attention. The politicians and ﬁnanciers of
the project, along with the general public, will all demand for certainty of the project budget in
terms of cost and duration.
Figure 1.2: Archaeological excavation at the Kongens Nytorv site.
3
==PAGE:16==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Finally, the public opinion has a heavy inﬂuence on the development of the project, because it is
virtually in everybody’s backyard. Hence, the public should be kept informed correctly and oﬀered
the possibility to voice its opinion and give input to the project. Additionally, every eﬀort should
be made to guarantee the public safety at all times. This way the project can be accepted by the
public and the huge negative eﬀects of a potentially adverse public opinion are minimized.
1.3
The Copenhagen Metro
The Copenhagen Metro is a metro system including 22 stations of which nine are underground, as
shown in Figure 1.3 The present system consist of three coherent lines stretching from Vanløse over
Frederiksberg and from Central Copenhagen to Christianshavn. After Christianshavn the Metro
line splits into two separate lines. One line, M1, is running South through Ørestaden terminating
at Vest Amager at the Control and Maintanice Center (CMC)of the metro system. The second
line, M2, runs South East along Amager Strandpark terminating at Kastrup Lufthavn (Copenhage
Airport). The Copenhagen Metro provide transportation to a daily number of approx. 160000
commuters and generates a turnover of approx. 0.6 Billion DKK corresponding to a marked sheer
of 18% of the public transportation in the Greater Copenhagen region [MS].
Figure 1.3: Present lines of the Copenhagen Metro System.
The ﬁrst construction stage of the Copenhagen Metro, the stage from Nørreport to Lergravsparken
and Vestamager stations was inaugurated on the 19th of October 2002. Stage 2A running from
Nørreport to Frederiksberg Stations opened in May 2003 and stage 2B from Frederiksbegr staion
to Venløse opened up in October 2003. The ﬁnal stage, stage 3, which prolongs the South East-
ern ﬁnger from Lergravsparken to Copenhagen Airport Kastrup was inaugurated in Septembere 2007.
In 2010 the construction of the fourth stage, the Ciryringen, was launched.
4
==PAGE:17==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Figure 1.4: Present and future lines of the Copenhagen Metro System.
At the time of its expected inauguration in 2019 the Cityringen will connect the inner city area to
the Østerbro area, the Nørrebro area, the Frederiksberg area and the Vesterbro area. It will also
connect the København Cental Station with the existing metro stations at Frederiksberg Station
and Kongens Nytorv Station.
The Ciryringen it self will consist of 15.5km long twin tubed underground metro line includ-
ing 17 underground stations situated at a depth of down to 35m.
Two appendices to the Cityringen is planed as well. One branch connecting the planned Nordhavnen
residential area and one branch connecting the existing Sydhavnen area to the Cityringen. The
Nordhavnen branch is expected to be inaugurated 2021 and the Sydhavne branch is expected to be
inaugurated in 2023. The present and future lines of the Copenhagen Metro System is presented in
Figure 1.4.
As mentioned previously, the Cityringen will consist of two twin tunnels running besides each other.
The tunnels will be drilled by the four Earth Pressure Balanced Tunnel Boring Machines Nora,
Tria, Eva and ﬁnally Minerva.
The tunnels of the Cityringen consist of a tunnel segment lining.
This lining holds a outer
diameter of 5.5m and an inner diameter of 4.9m. The segments have a thickness of 0.3m and
one segment ring consists of six diﬀerent segments A to F, where segment A is the key segment.
Figure 1.5 presents a principal sketch of a given tunnel lining ring utilized in the tunnelling of the
Cityringen.
5
==PAGE:18==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Figure 1.5: Dimensions of the tunnel lining.
1.4
TBM excavation versus shaft construction.
A particular challenge of constructing underground railway systems in highly urbanized areas is the
relation between the TBM tunnel excavation and the construction of the future station shafts.
A specially constructed shaft or a future station shaft may often serve as a launching point
or ﬁnishing point of the mechanized excavation. However, all the intermediate stations are also
subjected to the passage of the TBM. Overall, two types of TBM passage exist [1].
• "Void" crossing, where the station has already been build, at least in concern to the excavation
and the main structures.
• " Full" crossing, where the station volume has yet to be excavated.
In general, the most common method for TBM crossing of stations is that of void crossing. However,
the experience and technological progress of recent years in mechanized excavation have shown
that, once the excavation operations are successfully under way, the average tunnel advancement
that can be achieved can be very high. In many cases the construction of the stations can become
more critical than the tunnelling in relation to the time schedule.
If it would happen that during the construction of a given underground railway line, that the tunnel
6
==PAGE:19==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
construction is ahead of the construction of the approaching shaft, slowing down is necessary. In the
worst case, the TBM advancement has to be stopped in front of the shaft since it is not completely
equipped for the TBM to pass through
Such an event should be avoided at all costs, not only because the of the risks connected to
the consequent delays in the work schedule. But also because of the more general problems of
securing the tunnel safety. A TBM which has stopped, perhaps under a building or a road, is
evidently more dangerous than a functioning and operative TBM.
An other important issue to address is the actual break-in situation. When the TBM breaks
through the retaining wall of the shaft and into the open air void of the shaft, the TBM will suﬀer
an immediate loss of support pressure.
After the break-in into the void there is obviously no front formation to control. However, the void
between the shield and the soil formation is now unprotected due to the loss of support pressure.
The soil formation behind the cutter-head is only fully secure when the entire shield has entered
the shaft and the ring segments are situated all the way into the shaft and fully grouted as well.
Having in mind that underground stations are usually constructed in highly urbanized areas, a
major challenge awaits in relation to settlement control in the break-in area of the station shafts.
1.5
Settlements control at break-in
Several approaches to settlement control and management exist in order to handle the risk of
settlements in the break-in situation. However, in the following the focus will be kept on the
following two approaches.
• Injection of a grout-plug
• Construction of a break-in box.
In a suitable area before the retaining wall of the shaft and above the planned tunnel stretch a
grouting-plug can be constructed.
Figure 1.6: Plane view of the grouting area at Sønder Boulevard shaft.
7
==PAGE:20==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
In this particular area a number of holes can be drilled in order to facilitate the injection of grout
through the holes and in to the soil formation. Figure 1.6 presents an overview of the grouting area
at the break-in area of the Sønder Boulevard shaft. The drill holes are marked by red, blue and green.
The drill holes must reach below the lining of the tunnel in order to contribute to the settle-
ment management. Figure 1.7 presents a cross-section of the grout-plug in the break-in area of the
Sønder Boulevard shaft.
Figure 1.7: Cross-section of the grouting area at Sønder Boulevard shaft.
A grout-plug will enhance the strength of the soil formation and thus, reduce the risk of settlements
in the break-in situation. However, other advantages of a grout-plug exist. If the given soil consists
of a loose soil material, the grout will contribute to stabilizing the given soil material. An other
advantage of the grouting is the fact that the grout will penetrate in to the pores of the given soil
8
==PAGE:21==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
and thus, reduce the permeability of the soil. This will add to the settlement management through
a better control of the ground water and prevent the ground water from seeping in to the working
chamber of the TBM.
The other opportunity is to construct a break-in box inside the actual shaft. Figure 1.8 presents
the break-in box constructed in the Frederiksberg Station shaft.
CMT VFC on 13/09/2014
Figure 1.8: Longitudinal section of the break-in box at Frederiksberg Station shaft.
The break-in box allows the TBM to break through the retaining wall of the shaft. Continuing
fully pressurized in to the concrete box until the entire shield is inside the box and the lining of the
tunnel is situated and secured all the way to the lining wall of the station shaft. When the shield
is completely inside the break-in box and the tunnel lining is situated correctly the face support
pressure is removed and the excavation is stopped.
When applying a break-in box, the eminent danger of settlements in the break-in situation,
is reduced signiﬁcantly because the drop in support pressure can be controlled and the support
pressure can be reduced gradually. This is in contrast to a break-in into a open shaft which will
cause an instantaneous and uncontrolled drop in support pressure.
1.6
Scope and methodology
No oﬃcial code or clearly deﬁned methodology exists in relation to determine the needed face
support pressure and preforming the settlement control of a TBM excavated tunnel, especially in
relation to the break-in situation.
It is the scope of this thesis to preform a schematic analysis of two already performed TBM
break-in cases in order to predict the expected settlements and needed face support pressure of
a future TBM break-in at a speciﬁc underground station shaft. Additionally it is the aim of the
thesis to discuss and make suggestions to the settlements management of this speciﬁc station.
The two already performed break-in cases are the break-in at the Sønder Boulevard shaft and the
break-in at the Frederiksberg Station shaft. The Sønder Boulevard case is chosen due to the fact
that the tunnels are excavated in two diﬀerent levels and thus, the break-ins are performed at two
diﬀerent depths. The break-ins at Frederiksberg Station are performed at the same depth, but in a
9
==PAGE:22==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
mixed soil formation. From the knowledge obtained by these two cases an attempt to predict the
stability issues of the Rådhuspladesen Station will be carried out.
As a common feature of all three cases, the analysis will be performed in a lower bound situ-
ation and a upper bound situation. In the lower bound case the analysis is preformed applied the
smallest obtained soil properties and the highest ground water level. The upper bound analysis is
preformed applying the largest obtained soil properties and the lowest ground water level. In order
to determine the eﬀect of the grout-plug the properties of the grout-plug is calculated using the
principle of Voigt and Reuss, in which the unit weight of grout is set to 24kN/m3 and the Young’s
modulus of the grout is set to 30GPa.
The analysis is preformed in six diﬀerent stages.
1 Analytical calculations of settlements
2 2D Numerical calculations of settlements
3 Calculation of face pressure
4 3D Numerical calculations of settlements
5 Presentation of recorded settlements
6 Comparison
The presentation of the recorded settlements is only valid for the two already performed cases and
not for the third and future case.
1.6.1
Analytical Calculations of Settlements
In order to perform an analytical calculation of the settlements the method suggested by Jaeger and
Cook under the assumption of far ﬁeld stresses is applied[2]. The method of Jaeger and Cook will
deliver reliable results in relation to the vertical displacements on the ground surface. However, it
will not provide meaningful results in relation to the displacements at the vicinity of the tunnel lining.
Four diﬀerent analytical calculations will be preformed at four diﬀerent ground level coordinates.
The coordinates appear on Figure 1.9
Figure 1.9: Coordinates applied to the analytical and 2D analysis.
10
==PAGE:23==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Please note that the Y-coordinate varies from case to case in relation to the ground and tunnel
level.
1.6.2
2D Numerical Calculations of Settlements
The 2D calculations are performed using the FEM-program PLAXISTM 2D.
In contrast to the analytical calculations, the results of numerical calculation are obtained as
total displacements. This is due to a number of facts.
1 The total displacements will produce a visually more detailed distribution pattern compared
to the pattern of the vertical displacements.
2 At the ground level the vertical displacements, obtained by PLAXISTM 2D, are in magnitude
of 10−3m. In contrast the horizontal displacements are in order of magnitude of 10−6m. The
numbers tabulated after each analysis are rounded numbers. Hence, the contribution from
the horizontal displacements are negligible and thus, comparable to the analytically obtained
vertical displacements.
3 The total displacements at the vicinity of the tunnel consist of considerable contributions
from both vertical and horizontal displacements.
In order to provide a comparison, results from the 2D analysis are obtained at the same coordinates
as presented on Figure 1.9.
1.6.3
Calculation of Face Pressure
Initially the needed face support pressure is calculated using the suitable approach. In the SBV
and FB cases the calculated face pressure is compared to the actual applied face support pressure.
However, in order to compare the applied face pressure to the calculated face pressure, the
obtained face pressure data must be treated in order to facilitate a visual comparison. The face
pressure is induced into the formation through six diﬀerent nozzles just behind the cutting wheel.
Two of these nozzles are situated in the top of the head, two in the middle of the head and the last
two nozzles are situated at the bottom of the head.
For each segment the data locker measure the face pressure applied each nozzle through out
the entire excavation. Figure 1.10 present the raw data collected during the excavation from Aksel
Møllers Have to Frederiksberg Station.
It is apparent that the raw data appear in a visually very confusing manner. In order to be
able to compare the obtained data with the calculations the raw data must be treated in the
following manner.
• The abscissa of the raw data is per segment number. Due to the curvature of the excavated
tunnel the total length of the segment rings is longer than the chainage length. Therefore,
the length of the segments has to be converted in order to ﬁt the chainage length.
• The calculations are preformed in steps of 20m. Therefore, the average value of the raw data
is summarized for each 20m.
• In order to provide a better visual appearance the raw data is presented by a average value
and a maximum and a minimum value.
The treated raw data is presented in Figure 1.11.
11
==PAGE:24==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.

1.6.4
3D Numerical Calculations of Settlements
The 3D calculations is performed using the FEM-program PLAXISTM 3D.
The reference coordinates used in the analysis is presented in Figure 1.12.
12
==PAGE:25==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Figure 1.12: Coordinates applied the 3D analysis.
Please note that the Y-coordinate remain the same but the Z-coordinate varies from case to case in
relation to the ground and tunnel level. The X-coordinate is the same as in the 2D cases. Like in
the 2D case the results of the 3D analysis are obtained as total displacements. The reasons for this
are the same as mentioned in the 2D case. it should be mentioned that the vertical displacements
at ground level, obtained by 3D analysis, are in the order of magnitude of 10−3m whereas the
displacements in the two horizontal directions are in a order of magnitude of 10−6m. This is similar
to the 2D analysis.
1.6.5
Recorded Settlements
In order to verify or disregard the results of the diﬀerent calculations methods recorded settlement
data of the SBV and FB case are presented. Monitoring point close to the break-in area are chosen
in order to provide a comparison.
1.6.6
Comparison
Finally the results of the analysis stages are collected and compared. The comparison will provide
the basis for a discussion and for future suggestions.
13
==PAGE:26==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
2
Tunnel Boring Machines.
As an alternative to drilling and blasting (D&B) methods in rock and conventional manual ex-
cavation in soil, Tunnel Boring Machines (TBM) are commonly used in today’s infer-structural
tunnelling and mining projects [1]. TBMs have the advantages of limiting the disturbance to the
surrounding ground and producing a smooth tunnel wall. By both preforming the actual excavation
and installing the tunnel lining in a continues process the cost of lining the tunnel can be reduced
signiﬁcantly and makes the TBM tunnelling, speciﬁcally suitable in heavily urbanized areas, as the
risk of settlements and thus, harm to adjacent structures can be reduced. A major disadvantage of
TBM excavation is the upfront cost. TBMs are relative expensive to construct, and can be diﬃcult
to transport. However, as modern tunnels become longer, the cost of tunnel boring machines versus
drill and blast is actually reduced. This is due to the fact that tunnelling with TBMs is much more
eﬃcient and results in shortened completion times.
Modern TBMs typically consist of the rotating cutting wheel, called a cutter head, followed
by a main bearing, a thrust system and trailing support mechanisms. The type of machine used
depends on the particular geology of the project, the amount of ground water present and various
other factors. In the following, hard rock TBM excavation will not be considered, instead the focus
will be on soft ground TBM excavation in saturated soils.
2.1
Operative principles
Tunnel construction in saturated soils is being carried out even more frequently using closed shields
TBM’s, which allows the control of surface settlements and limit the risk of tunnel face failure
through the continued support of the face during excavation [3]. Mainly, two diﬀerent methods of
exploiting the closed shield of the TBM exist:
• Slurry-shield (SS) tunnelling.
• Earth Pressure Balanced (EPB)-shield tunnelling.
The slurry-shield method use a bentonite suspension as support ﬂuid, Figure 2.1A. The excavation
chamber is situated directly behind the cutter-head and separation by a submerged wall from
the working chamber. During the advancement of the slurry-shield TMB the excavation chamber
is completely ﬁlled with bentonite and the working chamber behind is approximately two-thirds
ﬁlled. The two chambers are connected in the form of communicating pipes via an opening in the
submerged wall. A ﬁlter cake is created ahead of the cutting wall by pressurising the bentonite in
the working chamber by compressed air. The bentonite penetrates the ground to form a membrane,
which holds back the groundwater.
BENTONITE
(a)
EXCAVATED SOIL
SCREW
CONVEYOR
p=0
(b)
Figure 2.1: Closed-shield types: (a) Slurry shield. (b) EPB-shield [3].
The actual excavation of the slurry-shielded TBM is quit similar to the excavation method of
a hyrdo-mill [4]. Additionally to supporting the excavation face, the bentonite also acts as a
14
==PAGE:27==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
suspension medium for excavated material. The dug out material falls into the excavation chamber
and the excavated material is pumped through the suction line running through the TBM and the
established tunnel to the separation plant at the surface. At the separation plant the excavated soil
is separated from the bentonite suspension and the clean suspension is transferred back into the
slurry circuit.
Earth pressure balanced (EPB) shields, Figure 2.1B, function on the principle of using the thrust
and forward movements of the TBM to maintain a pressure on the face. The face support pressure
is applied by utilizing the ground just excavated, collected and pressurized in the working chamber.
Figure 2.2 shows a basic sketch of an EPB shield with the components involved in the handling
of the support pressure, marked. The opening of the TBM cutter-head, which is equipped with
cutting tools such as discs or picks, permit the collection and accumulation of the excavated soil in
the working chamber. The spoil or muck collected in the working chamber is extracted from the
working chamber by the means of a rotating screw conveyor, or Archimedes screw.
 
Figure 2.2: Earth pressure balanced shield machine [Herrenknecht.de].
A dynamic equilibrium based on the balance of excavated and extracted volumes is created inside
the working chamber. Adjustments of this equilibrium, through variation of the screw-rotation
speed, makes it possible to create accumulation and consequent pressurization of the material
trapped in the working chamber. In short, the face support pressure is controlled by varying the
screw-rotation speed, as a function of the TBM penetration rate.
In addition to the basic function of muck extraction and control of face support pressure, the screw
conveyor allows the dissipation of the pressure in the working chamber, from its maximum value, at
bottom level of the chamber, to the atmospheric level at the discharge gate. This is done through
the formation of a "plug" of muck along the screw itself.
Finally, the longitudinal hydraulic jacks acting on the already positioned lining segments in-
side the tail shin of the shield exert a pushing force on the shield and the bulkhead. This pushing
force transfers an additional pressure to the ground that, together with the support pressure, must
be adequate for excavating and counteracting the friction forces on the shield and for supplying the
needed face support pressure.
In principle an EPB machine can operate in three available modes depending on the expected
stability of the tunnel face.
• Open mode - The excavation chamber is almost empty while excavating. Therefore, no
support is provided to the tunnel face. The excavation may be performed in the open mode
if the ground conditions assure a completely stable tunnel face, and the groundwater ﬂow
towards the face or into the excavation chamber does not cause any hydro-geological or
shield-operational problems.
15
==PAGE:28==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
• Transition mode - The basic feature of the transition mod is that the EPB shield is operated
with closed, however, only partially ﬁlled excavation chamber. The spoil in the excavation
chamber may be pressurized in the usual EPB way, as describe previously, or by means of
compressed air in order to reduce or avoid a groundwater inﬂow into the excavation chamber.
This mode is applicable for cases in which the formation at the face is stable and when the
risk of over-excavation at the tunnel face is low. The main purpose of the transition mode is
to control moderate water inﬂows through the front face of the EPB machine. Moreover, an
immediate change of operation mode to the closed mode is possible.
• Closed mode - The excavation chamber is completely ﬁlled with the excavated soil and is
pressurized. The applied pressure balances the acting earth and hydrostatic pressures. This
operation mode is required in unstable soft soils under groundwater table. Additionally, the
uncontrolled water inﬂow is prevented during the excavation.
2.2
Ground conditioning
In order to control the support pressure eﬃciently it is vital that the muck inside the working
chamber requires speciﬁc properties [4]. These properties can be obtained and adjusted by mixing
the spoil with various and suitable additives. The main purposes of the additives, or conditioning
agents, are to ensure the control of the face support pressure, facilitate the "plug" formation inside
the screw conveyor, and minimize the cutter head torque as well as minimizing the tear and wear
of the cutting tools.
 
Figure 2.3: Application range of the EPB shield[4].
The handling of the muck in the working chamber is mostly governed by the soil of the given
formation in which the tunnelling is preformed. Several condition agents exist such as: Slurries,
foams, ﬁllers, polymers etc. The condition agents can be introduced directly at the front, in the
excavation chamber or in the screw conveyor. A common feature of any conditioning agent is the
fact that they must be easy to handle and they must be completely non-toxic and biodegradable,
i.e. environmentally friendly
16
==PAGE:29==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Choosing the suitable agent depends on their physical and chemical properties and on the type
of soil, in which the tunnelling is preformed. The ﬁne-grain soil range, area 1 on Figure 2.3, is
regarded as the principal application range of the EPB shields. Within this range the spoil may be
conditioned only by water. When the excavation is preformed through coarser ground, area 2 on
Figure 2.3, the spoil has to be injected with a foam component in order to provide the support
pressure. The coarser ground is usually referred to as the secondary application range of the EPB
shield. When excavating in area 3 and area 4 of Figure 2.3 polymers have to be added into the
the excavation chamber, in addition to the foam, in order to provide the required properties of the
spoil.
When excavating in the principal and secondary application ranges it can be expected that
the EPB machine deliver its best performance. Both in regard to the tunnelling distance but
also in regard to keeping the aﬀected soil formation stable and avoiding settlements surface level.
Excavation in area 4 and beyond becomes equally challenging as the soil gets loser and thus, less
stable and the risk of over-excavation becomes more apparent.
2.3
Field of application
It is not possible to precisely deﬁne which ground and soil conditions are suitable and not suitable
for TBM excavation [1]. In general it is widely accepted to diﬀer between two diﬀerent ground
conditions. One category of soil that can be readily excavated using a Slurry Shield machine. A
second category of soil of which it is advantageous to utilize a EPB machine. Such a division is
usually made by comparing the particle size distributions of the various soils to be excavated in a
standard grain size distribution diagram. The discriminating factor of such a comparison is the
content of ﬁne grains in the soil.
In choosing the right type of machine there are some theoretical aspects, related to the typi-
cal functioning principles of the two diﬀerent types of machines. These aspect which need to be
considered are:
• Slurry Shield Machines apply the face support pressure through the formation of a "cake"
between the slurry and the ground. The higher the ground permeability, the more diﬃcult is
the formation of the cake. Therefore, there must be an upper limit to the particle size of the
ground in the face to be excavated, which is strictly related to the permeability. Additionally,
the ﬁner the particle size, the more problematic the functioning of separation plant will get.
As a consequent there must be a lower limit to the particle size from an operational point of
view.
• Earth Pressure Balanced Machines apply the support pressure directly to the face using
the resulting muck. The face support pressure can be managed when the material in the
working chamber is transformed into a "paste" or muck by adding certain additives as discussed
in the previous section.
Furthermore, one of the most important features for functioning in EBP mode is to create a pressure
gradient in the screw conveyor, in order to dissipate the pressure values at the bottom of the
working chamber to the atmospheric pressure at the discharge gate while still avoiding potential
water inﬂow. This is only possible if the muck moving into the screw has an acceptable composition
and permeability.
Without ground conditioning, the EPB application is problematic in relatively loose grounds
and in the presence of a small percentage, 5-10%, of ﬁne particles. On the other hand, SS could
have some diﬃculties in material having high to very high permeability. A common challenge for
both types is the presence of boulders with dimensions or quantities that may be too large to be
handled by the machines.
Recent experiences, i.e. the Turin metro, has demonstrated that, with the appropriate addi-
17
==PAGE:30==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
tion of soil conditioner, EPB machines can be eﬃciently applied even in grounds outside its
theoretically applicable ﬁeld.

For instance, in coarse soil, the addition of ﬁllers, such as bentonite or very ﬁne sand, can solve the
problem of pressure dissipation along the screw conveyor. In other types of soil, a heavy use of
other conditioning agents such as polymers, water and foams may be required. Figure 2.4 illustrates
the application range of the EPB machine and the appropriate additives of the various soils. Based
on Figure 2.4 it is possible to state that the EPB technology has a wide application range, but also
a speciﬁc operation limit. Additionally, there are economic limits linked to the cost of heavy use of
agents, which needs to be considered.
2.4
TBM of Copenhagen Cityringen
Four Earth Pressure Balanced Tunnel Boring Machines are utilized in the construction of the
Copenhagen Cityringen. These machines are manufactured by the Italian manufacture S.E.L.I.
SpA. Below, Figure 2.5, a cross-section of one of the applied S.E.L.I machines is displayed. A more
detailed description of the S.E.L.I. EPB shield machine can be seen in Appendix ??.
The machine consist mainly of the following features:
1 The cutter head
2 Front shield or ﬁrst articulation
3 Stationary shield or second articulation
4 Tail shield or third articulation
The ﬁrst and most prominent part is the cutter-head at the front. The cutter-head holds a diameter
of 5780mm and is equipped with 34 17" disc-cutters situated diagonally on the front of the head.
Additionally 12 disc-cutters and scrapers are applied the cutter-head as well. The cutter-head can
operate at a maximum speed of 5rpm using a power supply of 1200kW. The maximum torque limit
is 4790kNm which is achieved at 2.3rpm, whereas the minimum torque limit is 2180kNm, achieved
at the maximum power limit of 5.0rpm.
18
==PAGE:31==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
The front shield holds a diameter of 5740mm and a length of 2600mm. The working chamber is
situated in the front shield as well as the start of the screw conveyor.
Figure 2.5: Cross section of TBM utilized at the Copenhagen Cityring project [SELITunneling.it].
The stationary shield is situated at the middle of the machine, and holds a diameter of 5740mm and
a length of 3700mm. Finally, the shield consists of the tail shield, again with a diameter of 5740mm
but with a length of 3600mm. It is in the tail shield that the segment rings are installed and trust
force is applied. Additionally, it is from the tail shield that the grout is induced backwards into the
void between the ground formation and the outer diameter of the segment rings.
The three shield parts form a total shield length of 9900mm. The three shield parts acts as
three diﬀerent articulations enabling the shield to bend and turn in order to facilitate a variation in
the tunnel lining. The particular shield dimensions allow the S.E.L.I TBM to preform a curved
lining with a horizontal radius of 190m. The possible vertical radius of the S.E.L.I TBM is 625m
leading to a maximum tunnel gradient of ±6 %. The S.E.L.I TBM can handle a maximum face
support pressure of 4.5bar and holds a maximum advancement rate of 80mm/min.

3
Face Pressure Calculations
In the following section a number of diﬀerent approaches and methods of calculating the needed
face pressure will be presented and discussed. It is the aim of this section to present the diﬀerent
methods in a tangible manner in order to present the basic of further calculations. A comprehensive
theoretical discussion is not the purpose of this section.
Since the emergence of the modern Tunnel Boring Machines after the Second World War a
number of authors have presented their approaches and suggestions on how to estimate the needed
face support pressure. Authors such as Broms & Bennemark, 1967, Atkinson & Protts, 1977,
Krause, 1987 have all presented carefully prepared methods to derive the support pressure. However,
as TBM’s becomes more advanced and their ﬁeld of application becomes wider, the methods of the
above mentioned authors may have lost their relevance and other more comprehensive methods
have been developed.
In the following four diﬀerent, and perhaps more contemporary approaches will be presented.
3.1
Method of Leca and Dormieux, 1990.
In 1990 Leca and Dormieux [5] introduced a method of how to address the stability of a shallow
tunnel driven in a Mohr-Coulomb material using a limit analysis approach. The major challenges
of such a problem originates from the fact that it is a fully three-dimensional situation. Leca and
Dormieux present both an upper bound solution as well as a lower bound solution allowing a
bracketed estimate to be made of the stability conditions of the given tunnel front.
According to Leca and Dormieux the construction of a shallow tunnel can be idealized as shown in
Figure 3.1.
Figure 3.1: Simplieﬁed geometry for the front stability of a shallow tunnel.
The soil, holding a unit weight of γ and a surcharge of σs, is applied at the ground surface. The
unsupported length behind the tunnel face is refereed to as P and the value of P is assumed to be
zero, which is a reasonable assumption for shield driven tunnels. A support pressure σT is applied
at the front face of the tunnel and in the following this support pressure is assumed to be constant.
The ground conditions are also assumed to be uniform around the vicinity of the advancing
tunnel. The soil is modelled as a Mohr-Coulomb material characterized by its cohesion c′ and its
friction angle φ′. For such material the unconﬁned compressive strength can be derived by Equation
20
==PAGE:33==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
3.1.
σc = 2 · c′ · cos φ′
1 −sin φ′
(3.1)
The related Rankine earth pressure coeﬃcients can be derived by Equation 3.2 and Equation 3.3,
for the active and passive cases respectively.
KA = 1 −sin φ′
1 + sin φ′
(3.2)
KP = 1 + sin φ′
1 −sin φ′
(3.3)
3.1.1
Limit Analysis.
The purpose of the limit analysis is to provide an estimate of the stability conditions for a mechanical
system regardless of the behaviour of the material it consist of. Stability conditions for the system
are derived in terms of the loads that can be applied to the system without causing the system
to fail. An upper bound estimate of such load is found by considering a kinematic admissible
failure mechanism characterized by having applied a force Pc exceeding the power Pv which can
be dissipated into the system. In contrast, the lower bound solution is represented by any set of
loads for which a stress ﬁeld can be found, that satisﬁes the equilibrium and the material yield criteria.
Stability can be characterized by the domain K of the supported loads.
The domain K is
bracketed by a lower bound domain K −and an upper bound domain K +. This is illustrated in
Figure 3.2 showing a system applied two sets of loads Q1 and Q2.
Figure 3.2: Bracketed estimate of the domain of supported loads.
Any set of loads located inside K −is potentially supported by the system while any set of loads
outside K + is unstable. The domain K is convex, meaning that if two sets of loads Q’ and Q”
are supportable, any linear combination of these loads λQ’+(1-λ)Q”, where 0 < λ ≤1, is also
supportable. Considering Figure 3.2, any point M located on the straight line between A and A’ is
inside K −as long as A and A’ both belong to K −. In order to preform the analysis of the tunnel
problem in accordance with the Leca and Dormieux method, three loading parameters need to
be considered: σs/σc, σT /σc and γD/σc, in which σs is the surcharge stress, σc is the unconﬁned
compressive strength derived by Equation 3.1 and σT is the needed support pressure.
21
==PAGE:34==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Figure 3.3: Discontinuity velocity along the
failure surface.

Coulomb criterion, the failure mechanisms must be

3.1.2
Upper bound solution.
In order to provide an upper bound solution Leca and
Dormieux considered three diﬀerent failure mecha-
nisms. All three mechanisms involve the moment of
a solid conical block with circular cross-sections, as
seen on Figure 3.4a. The opening of each cone block is equal to 2φ’ and its velocity V is parallel
to it’s axis. Thus, the condition of Equation 3.4 is satisﬁed along the failure surface between the
moving block and the rest of the ground.
(a) Conical blocks and kinematic conditions used in
MI, MII and MIII.
(b) Area of failure at the tunnel face
Figure 3.4: Conical blocks and kinematic conditions (a)and area of failure (b).
The three failure mechanisms MI, MII and MIII are shown in Figure 3.5. MI and MII are both
collapse mechanisms, whereas MIII describes the blow-out situation. Although the safety against
collapse is the main concern during tunnelling, the case of MIII may be relevant for shallow tunnels
excavated in weak soil, when the applied support pressure can exceed the strength of the soil
formation and lead to heaving of the soil in front of the cutter-head.
According to Figure 3.5a, describing failure mechanism MI, failure is due to the collapse of one
conical block. Considering the failure mechanism of MI, it can be seen that the failure mechanism
is characterized by only one parameter, the angle α between the axis of the cone adjacent to the
tunnel face and the horizontal axis.

Figure 3.5: Principal sketch of failure mechanism MI (a), failure mechanism MII (b) and blow-out
mechanism MIII (c).
In contrast to MI, the failure mechanism of MII, Figure 3.5b, consist of two conical blocks, and the
geometry of MII is slightly more complex compared to MI. The ﬁrst cone, block 1, is similar to the
cone of MI, however it is truncated by a plane π perpendicular to the plane of symmetry of the
tunnel, which projects as ∆. The second cone, block 2, is a mirror image of the ﬁrst cone with
respect to plane π’. π’ is perpendicular to π and runs through the centre of the intersection P

π intersects both blocks along the same elliptical surface P

the symmetry axis of the second cone is vertical. By doing so, MII, like MI, is only characterized
by the parameter α. The blow-out mechanism of MII, Figure 3.5c, is also characterized by α and
the geometry is similar to that of MI, except that the cone is inverted and the velocity is reversed.
It should be noted, that for all three mechanism, the intersection of the tunnel with its adjacent
cone is an ellipse P
01, as seen on Figure 3.4b, with the major semi-axis length equal to D/2. This
implies that only a part of the tunnel face is failing.

given that in order for the load sets, σs/σc, σT /σc and γD/σc to be stable, Pc and Pv must satisfy

3.1.3
Lower Bound Solutions.
A lower bound solution for the Mohr-Coulomb material has been suggested by Leca and Dormieux
as well. The lower bound solution is based on three stress ﬁelds, SI, SII and SIII as seen in Figure 3.6.
SI is a geostatic stress ﬁeld which can be used in the general case of a soil holding a weight
of γ > 0. In the geometry of SI, shown on Figure 3.6a, the ground is divided into three layers.
Above and below the tunnel (y ≥D/2ory ≤−D/2) where the stress ﬁeld is isotropic. In the layer
located at tunnel depth (−D/2 ≤y ≤D/2) the horizontal stress component along Oz is equal to σT .
SII and SIII, both apply to a weightless soil γ = 0.
Such an assumption may not be appli-
cable to a real life situation. However, the lower bound values, derived by SII and SIII, may
contribute to a wider understanding of the given soil and thus, contribute to an overall optimization
of the design as well as the construction solution.
The stress ﬁeld SII, Figure 3.6b, is axisymmetric around the tunnel axis. Inside the cylinder
C1, which extends the tunnel in the Oz direction, the axial stress is equal to σT and the radial and
tangential stresses are equal to a constant value of σ0, which is chosen such that the ground is at
yield everywhere in C1. Outside the cylinder C2, which is tangential to the ground surface, the
stress ﬁeld is isotropic and equal to σs. Between the two cylinders, C1 and C2, a solution for the
radial stress σr and the tangential stress σθ is derived from solving the equations of equilibrium
with the assumption that the soil is at yield, as seen in Appendix I.
SIII is spherically symmetrical around the centre O of the tunnel face. The stresses are isotropic
inside sphere S1 and outside S2, where they are equal to σT and σs respectively. Between S1 and
S2 the radial stress σr and both tangential stresses σθ and σφ are determined in the same way as
for SII by solving the equations of equilibrium with the assumption the the soil is at yield. Again
the derivations can be seen in Appendix I. Utilizing that SI, SII and SIII satisfy the equations of
equilibrium and the boundary conditions of the present problem, the solution can be summarized
in the following inequalities. For the stress ﬁeld SI, Expression 3.11 and Expression 3.12 are valid.

3.2
Method of Jancsecz and Steiner, 1994.
In order to ﬁnd the right support pressure for a tunnel face in granular soils, it is necessary to
consider the forces acting on a possible and probable failure model [6]. Similar to other solutions
in soil mechanics, the application of limit equilibrium analysis can deliver a limit earth pressure
acting on the face of a progressing tunnel. The selection of an appropriate factor of safety assisted
by deformation and/or settlement control allows determination of a support pressure which is not
too high nor to low. If the support pressure is too low, face collapse or extreme settlements may
occur. If, on the other hand, the support pressure is too high excessive air penetration with surface
upheaval may occur together with the loss in eﬀective face support, leading to a collapse in the
tunnel face.
The method of Jancsecz and Steiner is based on the three dimensional limit equilibrium model
displayed in Figure 3.10.
Figure 3.10: The tunnel-face stability model of the method of Jancsecz and Steiner.
The three dimensional failure model consists of two parts; a soil wedge (lower part) and a soil silo
(upper part) above the wedge. The vertical pressure resulting from the silo, and acting on the
31
==PAGE:44==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
horizontal surface of the soil wedge, will be determined separately from the lower part. According
to Terzaghi’s solution the vertical pressure is given by Equation 3.27.

Ka = 1 −sin φ

U and F are the circumference and area of the wedge from the horizontal plane view.
Utilizing the vertical pressure the weight of the silo, Gs, can be determined by Equation 3.29,
Gs = b · D · coth β · σ′

Figure 3.11 shows the variation of the slip angle β over the friction angle φ and ratio between the

Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Figure 3.11: Variation of the slip angle β.
Considering Figure 3.11, it can be seen that the inﬂuence of the overburden pressure on the slip
angle in relatively small, particularly if the ratio between the overburden and the tunnel diameter
exceeds 2. The main contribution to the variation of the slip angle derives from the angle of friction.
A dominant imitation to the method of Jancsecz and Steiner is the inﬂuence of the cohesion.
Already in Equation 3.27 the cohesion is the determining factor. If the cohesion exceeds a value of
more than 6.5kPa a negative result will be obtained and thus, the result becomes meaningless.
3.3
Anagnostou and Kovàri, 1996.
The method presented by Anagnostou and Kovàri is perhaps the most widely adopted method. The
method is applicable to a majority of cases, and provides meaningful ﬁrst hand estimates of the
required support pressure. These ﬁrst hand estimates can be used in order to determine where the
soil, in question, is competent or if a more comprehensive, and possibly cumbersome, investigation
of the soil stability is required.
33
==PAGE:46==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
 
Figure 3.12: Sliding mechanism according to Horn.
The method of Anagnostou and Kovàri is based on the assumed sliding mechanism presented by
Horn [3]. Figure 3.12 displays a principal sketch of the silo and sliding mechanism according to Horn.
EXCAVATED SOIL

As

An important issue of Anagnostou and Kovàri’s method is
the question of the inﬂuence of the pore water pressure, i.e.
the piezometric head hF in the work chamber, and what inﬂuence this may have on the stability of
the tunnel face.
34
==PAGE:47==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Figure 3.14: Seepage force f and eﬀective support pressure σT ’.
If hF is lower than the piezometric head h0 in the undisturbed state, the groundwater will seep
through the tunnel face. As a result the seepage force f will act towards the tunnel face and may
endanger the stability of the face.
Determination of the seepage forces requires a three-dimensional numerical seepage-ﬂow anal-
ysis. Figure 3.15 shows a typical result of a given three-dimensional numerical seepage-ﬂow analysis
displaying the contour-lines of the piezometric head in the plane of symmetry. The increasing
density of the potential lines close to the tunnel face indicates an increasing value of the seepage
force. As the seepage forces are oriented perpendicularly to the potential lines, the resultant seepage
force acting on the wedge slopes slightly downward, while that in the prism above is practically
vertical. Considering this issue, the destabilizing eﬀect of the seepage forces acting on the tunnel
face becomes apparent.
An approximately horizontal load is exercised on the wedge, while the vertical load from the
prism is simultaneously increased. The ﬁrst eﬀect is taken into account by introducing the seepage
forces acting within the wedge into its equilibrium equations. In order to determine the second
eﬀect, an iterative process is required.

==PAGE:48==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.

10 m

h = 30 m
20 m
Figure 3.15: Result of a given three-dimensional numerical seepage analysis.
3.3.1
Limit Analysis.
At limit equilibrium, the eﬀective support pressure σ′
T depends on the tunnel diameter D, the
overburden H, on the piezometric head in the chamber hF , on the elevation of the water table h0,
on the shear strength parameters c and φ, on the submerged unit weight γ′ and on the dry unit
weight γd for the soil below or above the water table. By preforming a dimensional analysis and by
taking into account the linearity of the equilibrium and failure equations, Anagnostou and Kovàri
obtained the following general form of the limit equation condition described in Equation 3.36.
σ′
T = F0γ′D −F1c + F2γ′∆h −F3c∆h/D
(3.36)
In Equation 3.36, F0 to F3 are dimensionless coeﬃcients depending on the friction angle, on the
ratio between the overburden and the tunnel diameter, H/D and (h0 −D)/D, and on the ratio of
the dry to the submerged unit weight γd/γ′. The curves utilized at determining the dimensionless
coeﬃcients F0 to F3 are displayed in Figure 3.16. The curves cover a relevant range of the geometric
parameters H/D and (h0 −D)/D. The ratio γd/γ′ is taken to 1.60, which is assumed to be a
good practical approximation. By combining Equation 3.36 and Figure 3.16 a simple but reliable
quantiﬁcation of the face stability at EPB-operation mode can be preformed.

Considering Figure 3.17 it is apparent that a relatively small friction angle requires an equally larger

level ∆h is equal to zero.
3.4
Broere, 2001
In 2001 Broere presented a method to calculate the needed face support pressure [7]. This method
addresses one of the most notable problems with in the previously introduced methods; the fact
that they are only suited for homogeneous soil only. Based on the work of Jancsecz and Steiner the
method of Broere provides a comprehensive approach to the calculation of face support pressure in
a multi layered soil formation.
When diﬀerent soils are present within the tunnel face, holding diﬀerent angels of friction, the force
equilibrium used in the models of Jancsecz and Steiner and Anagnostou and Kovàri are not valid.
In order to provide a method the can handle the challenges of a multi layered soil in a consistent
manner a stability model was established. Figure 3.18 displays the stability model created by
Broere. The model is based on the wedge part of the soil silo and wedge model presented by
Jancsecz and Steiner and shown in Figure 3.10 in a previous subsection.
39

Figure 3.18: Deﬁnition of symbols in the multi layered wedge model.
The wedge body of Figure 3.18 is subdivided into, N, smaller bodies of various thickness but with a
homogeneous soil in side the subdivided body. The soil conditions may vary between these slices, as
may the wedge angle ϑ(i) between the sloping failure plane of the i′th slice and the horizontal plane.
Each slice, i, is loaded by the resultant forces from the i −1 slice above and the i + 1 slice below,
Q(i)
a
and Q(i)
b
respectively, by the eﬀective weight of the slice itself G(i)
w and by the overburden
weight G(i)
s . At the slanted failure plane there is a cohesive force K(i) acting parallel to the plane,
as well as a friction force R(i), which results from the normal force N (i), working perpendicular to
the failure plane. The side faces of the wedge are each assumed to be loadedby the shear forces
T (i), acting in the same direction as K(i), against the deformation direction of the wedge. A force
equilibrium will yield the eﬀective earth force E(i) at the face which, in combination with the water
force W (i), is equal to the support force S(i).
In Equation 3.37 and Equation 3.38 the horizontal and vertical equilibrium is established re-
spectively.

For the case of a single slice wedge in a homogeneous soil the forces acting on the wedge can be cal-
culated by Equation 3.29 to Equation 3.32 deﬁned by Jancsecz and Steiner in the previous subsection.
From the obtained eﬀective earth force E the minimal required support pressure is calculated by
Equation 3.46 in the same manner as for the method of Jancsecz and Steiner.
σT = 4 · E
π · D2
(3.46)
As the method of Broere is based on the method of Jancsecz and Steiner, the method of Broere
suﬀer the same limitations as the method of Jancsecz and Steiner; the method cannot handle large
values of cohesion

4
PLAXISTM model
In the following section the conditions and inputs utilized in the numerical analysis section will be
described. The numerical analysis will be performed using the FEM-program PLAXISTM both in
the 2D application as well as the 3D application.
In order to provide a reliable comparison it has been the aim of this exercise to construct the models
as simple as possible and with as many common features possible between the 2D and 3D model.
A common feature between the two models is the attempt to include and simulate the diﬀerence in
diameter between the cutter-head and the shield. This is done by adding a calculation phase which
represents the contraction of the soil behind the advancing cutter-head.
An other important common feature is how the material properties of the soils are deﬁned in the
PLAXISTM models. Consequently the material model is kept as Mohr-Coulomb and drainage type
is kept as drained for all types of soil. In the parameters sections the unit weights, the Young’s
modulus, Poissons ratio, friction angel and cohesion are speciﬁed for the given soil. In the Flow
parameters, Interfaces and Initial sections the suggested default values are utilized.
It can be argued that it would be more correct to use the undrained soil state rather than
the drained state. The soil in contact with the advancing TBM will not be left unsupported for a
longer span of time, and the drained characters of the soil will not be expected to appear. However,
it is not possible to model the eﬀect of the soil contraction at the shield behind the head using
the undrained state. This is due to the presence of pore-water within the pores of the soil not
allowing the soil to contract. Having this in mind it is expected that settlements obtained by the
analyses will be slightly larger than settlements obtained by using the undrained state. Likewise it
is expected that soil patterns generated by the analyses will be relatively similar in the drained as
in the undrained state.
Finally, a common feature of the two models is the fact that the grouting of the void between soil
formation and the outer diameter of the tunnel segments has not been taken into account. This is
simply done in order to simplify and thus, limit the calculation time of the models.
4.1
2D model.
The 2D model utilized in this thesis is based on the oﬃcial PLAXISTM 2D tutorial describing a
phased excavation of a shield tunnel The calculations consist of four plastic phases, each of which
models the parts of the prevailing excavation process:
• Phase 0 - Initial phase: Here the initial soil parameters and water conditions are deﬁned.
• Phase 1 - Tunnelling: In this phase the tunnel lining is installed and the interface between
the lining and the soil formation is activated.
• Phase 2 - Contraction: Here the diﬀerence in diameter between the head and the shield is
simulated. This is done by deﬁning a 0.5% contraction of the lining.
• Phase 3 - Final lining: The ﬁnal lining is installed in this phase.
The actual model dimensions and soil conditions can be found in the appendices related to each
construction site.
4.1.1
Weakness of the 2D model
An obvious weakness of the 2D model is the fact that it only generates results in the 2D plane.
Results generated in the 2D plan are in many cases suﬃcing in order to preform a stability analysis.
42
==PAGE:55==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
However, face stability is a three dimensional problem. As a consequence, results obtained by 2D
analysis cannot directly be linked to a face stability problem.
4.2
3D model.
Like the 2D model the 3D model, utilized in this thesis, is based on the oﬃcial PLAXISTM 3D
tutorial describing a phased excavation of a shield tunnel. In order to model this a geometry
consisting of slices each 1.4 m long will be used. The calculations consist of three plastic phases,
each of which models the parts of the prevailing excavation process:
• The support pressure at the tunnel face, needed to prevent active failure at the face.
• The conical shape of the TBM shield.
• The excavation of the soil and the control of the pore water within the TBM.
• The installation of ﬁnal tunnel lining.
Figure 4.1: Construction stages of the shield tunnel model.
The model itself consist of a 25m long tunnel consisting of a concrete tunnel lining representing
the established tunnel lining. Next to the 25m long concrete tunnel a stretch of 11.2m is created.
This stretch is subdivided by 1.4m representing the length of each tunnel segment. At the ﬁrst
9.8m of these 11.2m a steel shield is deﬁned to representing the TBM. The remaining 1.4m of the
11.2m represents the soil to be excavated. The front of the shield is applied a vertical line load
which will represent the applied face support pressure. In order to simplify the model and to limit
the calculation time the inﬂuence of the trust forces has been neglected. A principal sketch of the
construction stages is displayed in Figure 4.1.
Unlike the 2D model the 3D only consist of three calculation stages.
• Phase 0 - Initial phase: Here the initial soil parameters and water conditions are deﬁned.
• Phase 1 - Positioning of the TBM: In this phase the concrete tunnel of the ﬁrst 25m is
activated. The steel shield is activated and so is the interface between the shield and the soil
formation. The shield is applied a contraction of 0.5% progressing from the front of the shield
to the back of the shield with a slope of -0.0667%. The given face support pressure is applied
to the front of the shield by deﬁning the given values to the already deﬁned line load at the
front of the shield.
43
==PAGE:56==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
• Phase 2 - TBM advancement: Here the shield is moved 1.4m forward in order to represent
the advancement of the TBM. The face pressure and contraction is deﬁned as in the previous
phase. Between the shield and the original concrete tunnel a 1.4m wide space is created. This
space is applied the same properties as the concrete tunnel in order to represent the build of
one segment ring.
Again this is illustrated on Figure 4.1
4.2.1
Weakness of the 3D model
A major objection to this simpliﬁed model is the fact the it only shows the inﬂuence of TBM
excavation on a pure soil formation. The model does not include the station shaft. It must be
assumed that the presence of the station shafts will have signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the soil pattern
and the ﬁnal settlements. However, it can be assumed that the presence of the station shaft will
dampen rather than amplify the settlements in a break-in situation.
An other signiﬁcant weakness of the stability of the PLAXISTM 3D model itself. Figure 4.2
displays the soil pattern of a successful performed calculation and an unsuccessful calculation

Figure 4.2: Successful and unsuccessful preformed calculations.
When considering the successful performed calculation of Figure 4.2a it can be seen that the
displacements are concentrated above the shield. The displacements at the front are of a smaller
magnitude compared to the displacements above the shield. This could indicate that the eﬀect
originating from the shield contraction is dominating over the applied face support pressure.
If the model is applied a relatively large face support pressure it is not possible to preform a
successful calculation. Figure 4.2b shows the soil pattern of an unsuccessfully performed calculation
and considering this ﬁgure it is apparent that no conclusion can be made from an unsuccessfully
preformed calculation. Thus, the use of this model does not facilitate any conclusions in regard to
which magnitudes of support pressure will lead to collapse at the face or will result in a blow-out
situation.
The displacements behind the shield is neglected in the following analysis as they are assumed to
originate from an interface issue between the newly placed tunnel segment and the TBM shield.
44
==PAGE:57==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Finally, it is important to stress an other weakness of this model. The model does not include the
inﬂuence of a loose soil and the permeability of the soil. Therefore, it is not possible to address the
issue of over excavation or blow-out directly.
45
==PAGE:58==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
5
Sønder Boulevard Shaft.
The Sønder Boulevard (SBV) construction site is situated in the Vesterbro part of Copenhagen.
Figure 5.1 provides an overview of the sits and its surroundings.
Figure 5.1: Plan view of the Sønder Boulevard transfer shaft.
The purpose of the SBV shaft is to serve as a transfer shaft. The transfer shaft allows the metro
trains to enter Cityringen from the Control and Maintenance Center (CMC) and from the future
Sydhavnen branch of the metro system.
In order to provide access to the main tracks of Cityringen and due to the lack of available
space, in the highly urbanized Vesterbro, the SBV shaft is constructed in two levels. The construc-
tion of a two levelled shaft allows the tracks from the CMC to intersect with the main tracks on
their own level. A scaled cross-section of the SBV shaft is available in Appendix III. Appendix III
also lists the information regarding the soil conditions as well as all the relevant information and
calculations. The result ﬁles from the PLAXISTM 2D and 3D analyses are available on the attached
USB-pen. Figures showing the generated mesh, utilized in the 2D as wall as the 3D analyses, are
available in Appendix III together with a sketch of the dimensions utilized in the FEM analysis.
The two levelled shaft requires the tunnel excavation to be performed in two diﬀerent levels;
one deep tunnel stretch and one shallow tunnel stretch.
The soil layer distribution at the vicinity of the shaft is seen on Figure 5.2 for the deep tunnel
stretch and on Figure 5.11 for the shallow tunnel stretch. The surface of the ground is approx. at
level +3m. From +3m and down to -1m a ﬁll layer (FY) is present. From -1m to -3m the soil
consists of a clay till (ML) and from -3m to -5m of a sandy clay till (MS). From -5m and downwards
to -8m the soil consists of Selandian Greensand (PS). The soil at -8m down to -10m consists of
high permeable Upper Copenhagen Limestone. (UCL(HP)). From -10m down to -15m a layer
of Upper Copenhagen Limestone (UCL) appear. At -15m and down to -35m the soil consists of
Middle Copenhagen Limestone and ﬁnally from -35m and downwards the soil consists of Lower
Copenhagen Limestone (LCL). The water table varies from approx. 0m down to -2m. All soil
properties are available in Appendix III.
46
==PAGE:59==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
In the following the two diﬀerent tunnel stretch will be presented and analysed in two diﬀer-
ent sections below.
5.1
Deep Tunnel Stretch
Figure 5.2 show a cross-section of the soil formation in which the deep tunnel stretch is excavated.
The excavation starts at the TGV at chainage 130853m and then proceeds on to the SBV at
chainage 130100m. The bottom lining of the deep tunnel is at -29m at the shaft break-in.
Figure 5.2: Tunnel lining of TGV-SBV stretch. Deep tunnel. Direction of drive is right to left.

5.1.3
Calculation of Face Pressure
A calculation of the needed face pressure has been preformed using the various methods presented
previously. Table 5.3 displays an overview of the various methods of face pressure calculation and
there applicability in this speciﬁc case.
49
==PAGE:62==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
Table 5.3: Applicability of the diﬀerent face pressure calculation approaches. SBV deep tunnel
Calculation

Upper bound
Approach
Ex. cohesion
Incl. cohesion
Ex. cohesion

Leca & Dormieux
N/A

Yes

N/A = Not Applicable. ∗To large friction angle and to large H/D ratio. ∗∗To large value
of soil cohesion.
For this particular soil it is not possible to obtain a meaningful solution applying the Leca and
Dormienux approach. The high value of cohesion and of friction angle of the MCL yield a very
high value of compressive strength σc which results in a negative value of support pressure in the
collapse case. In the blow-out case the large value of friction angle and the large value of the H/D
ratio result in Ny and Ns coeﬃcients approaching an inﬁnite value and thus, do not contribute to a
meaningful result. This is valid for both the upper bound and lower bound solution of the Leca
and Dormienux approach.
The high value of cohesion for the MCL is also challenging in relation to the Jancescz and
Steiner method. The vertical stress of the soil silo reach a negative value when the cohesion of the
MCL is applied. As the method of Broere is based on the work of Jancescz and Steiner the Broere
approach is also not applicable in this case.
The only approach to the support pressure issue that will provide meaningful results in the
case is the Anognostou and Kovàri approach. Assuming that the piezometric head hF in the
working chamber is similar to the ground water table and disregarding the cohesion, it is possible
to provide a reasonable estimate of the needed support pressure. As the MCL holds the same unit
weight in the upper bound as well in the lower bound case, the lower bound result will not vary
from the upper bound result.
Figure 5.5 show the calculated face support pressure along with the hydrostatic pressure and
the applied TBM face pressure in the actual excavation process.

Considering Figure 5.5 it is apparent that the applied face pressure at the start of the excavation to

the hydrostatic pressure. After chainage 130250m and to the end of the excavation, the face
pressure is kept at a relatively constant level of approx. 5% and 8.5% below the hydrostatic pressure
and the calculated support pressure.
Considering Figure 5.5 as a whole is seems reasonable to assume that it is the hydrostatic pressure
that govern the need of support pressure. This is supported by the fact that there is a drop in
the applied face pressure just before the break-in. This fact could indicate that the grout-plug
successfully have managed to reduce the permeability of the limestone and thereby, reducing the
need of ground water management.

assumed to be similar to the ground water table and the cohesion is disregarded.

230200m that the applied face pressure resembles either the calculated face pressure or the hydro-
static pressure. At the start of the excavation the applied face pressure diﬀers from the calculated
face pressure and the hydrostatic pressure by approx. 17% and approx. 24% respectively.

Before the break-in the applied face pressure diﬀers from the calculated face pressure and the
hydrostatic pressure by approx. 43% and approx. 36% respectively. Like in the deep tunnel case
there is a dramatic drop in the applied face pressure just before the break-in. This could indicate
that the grout-plug have succeed in reducing the permeability.
It is diﬃcult to explain exactly why the actual applied face pressure diﬀer so dramatically from the
calculated, especially having in mind that this was not the issue in deep tunnel case. A possible
explanation could be that there is a large aspect of human inﬂuence in steering a TBM.

6
Frederiksberg Station.
The Frederiksberg Station (FB) construction site is situated in the Frederiksberg part of Copenhagen.
Figure 6.1 provides an overview of the site and its surroundings.
Figure 6.1: Plan view of the Frederiksberg Station shaft.
Several challenges are to be dealt with in relation to the excavation of the tunnels and the break-in
at the Frederiksberg Station.
In contrast to the Sønder Boulevard shaft, the intention of the Frederiksberg Station shaft is
to serve as an actual stop on the Cityringen metro line. The tunnel lining of the Cityringen will
run underneath the already exciting tunnel lining of the M1 and M2 metro lines. The existing
metro lines run in a cut-and-cover tunnel with a base level approx. at level 0m. The existing
cut-and-cover tunnel provides a challenge to the TBM tunnelling due to the fact that the existing
tunnel is secured from upheaval by soil anchors. A number of these soil anchors are anchored at a
level which interfere with the tunnel lining of the Cityringen.
An other major challenge to tunnelling of the stretch just before and at the break-in at FB
is the presence of large structures in the vicinity of the station shaft. Considering Figure 6.1 it is
seen that the tunnel stretch runs just beneath the massive structure of Frederiksberg Shopping
Center. The presence of this large superstructure must be expected to inﬂuence the stability of the
excavation process.
Below, in Figure 6.2 presents a cross-section of the soil formation in which the tunnels are
excavated. Figure 6.2 also presents the soil conditions of the stretch as well as the above mentioned
superstructures.
65
==PAGE:78==

The tunnel stretches from the Aksel Møllers Have (AMH) Station at chainagen 111951m to the
Frederiksberg Station at chainage 111418m.
The surface of the ground is approx.
at level +11m.
From +11 and down to +9m the soil
consist of a ﬁll layer (FY) and from +9m downwards to +4m a mixture of clay till and sandy
clay (ML/MS) is present. From +4m down to +2m the soil consist of melt water sand and
gravel (DS/DG). At +2m downwards to -7m an other layer of ML/MS is present. From -7m to
-8m a high permeable Upper Copenhagen Limestone (UCL(HP)) is present. Upper Copenhagen
Limestone (UCL) is present at -8m down to -10m and ﬁnally from -10m and downwards the soil
consist of Middle Copenhagen Limestone (MCL). The ground water table varies from approx. -1m
and down to approx. -4m. The tunnels break-in at an approx. base level of -9m. The tunnel
face at the break-in is excavated in a mixture of approx. 50% ML/MS, 15% UCL(HP) and 35% UCL.
All the soil properties are available in Appendix IV. All relevant information and calculations
regarding FB is present in Appendix IV along with ﬁgures showing the dimensions and the generated
mesh utilized in the 2D and 3D analysis. The result ﬁles from the PLAXISTM 2D and 3D analysis
are available on the attached USB-pen.

6.3
Calculation of Face Pressure
A calculation of the needed face pressure has been preformed using the various methods presented
previously. Table 6.3 displays a overview of the various methods of face pressure calculation and
their applicability in the speciﬁc case of the FB site.
Table 6.3: Applicability of the diﬀerent face pressure calculation approaches. FB.
Calculation
