Upper bound
Approach

Incl. cohesion
Ex. cohesion
Incl. cohesion
Leca & Dormieux
N/A

Jancescz & Steiner
N/A

Yes

N/A = Not Applicable. ∗To large friction angle and to large H/D ratio. ∗∗To large value
of soil cohesion.
As seen in Figure 6.2, the main part of the tunnel is excavated in UCL(HP) or UCL. As discussed in
the SBV case it is only the Anagnostou and Kovàri approach that will provide meaningful results in
relation to face support pressure of excavation in the Copenhagen limestone. Again, the piezometric
head hF in the working chamber must be assumed to be similar to the ground water table and
the soil cohesion must be disregarded. When the tunnelling approaches the station box the soil
changes to a combination of limestone and clay. However, the cohesion of the ML/MS is still to
high to be included in calculations preformed by the Anagnostou and Kovàri method.
The large value of cohesion limestone and ML/MS also provides the limitation of the Jancescz and
Steiner method as well as the Broerer method. This is due to the fact that the vertical stresses of
the soil silo reach a negative value when the cohesion of UCL, UCL(HP) or ML/MS is applied.
The high value of cohesion and friction angles of the UCL and UCL(HP) yield to an extremely
high value of compressive strength which again yield to a negative value of support pressure in
the collapse case. In the blow-out case, the large value of friction angle and the large result of the
H/D ratio result in Ny and Ns coeﬃcients approaching an inﬁnite value and thus, do not provide
a meaningful result. This is valid for both the upper bound and lower bound solutions of the Leca
and Dormienux approach in both load cases. In relation to the ML/MS it is only possible to obtain
a positive value of compression strength in the upper bound situation but the H/D ratio leads
to a value of Ny that do not contribute to a meaningful result. Again, for the blow-out case, the
large value of friction angle and the large result of the H/D ratio result in Ny and Ns coeﬃcients
approaching an inﬁnite value leading to meaningless results. This is also valid for both the upper
bound and lower bound solutions of the Leca and Dormienux approach in both load cases.
Figure 6.4 shows the calculated face support pressure along with the hydrostatic pressure and the
applied TBM face pressure of the actual excavation process.
When considering Figure 6.4 it is apparent that there is very little correspondence between
the calculated support pressure and the actually applied face pressure. It is only at the area around
chainage 111600m to chainage 111800m that there is some trace of correspondence between the
calculated and the applied face pressure. However, the applied face pressure is still approx. 14% to
19% larger than the calculated support pressure and 31% to 35% larger than the hydrostatic pressure.
An other obvious discrepancy shown in Figure 6.4 is the fact that both the calculated support
pressure and the hydrostatic pressure peaks at the middle of the tunnel excavation where the
overburden is at its largest. This is in contrast to the actual applied face pressure which peaks just
after the break-out of AMH at chainage 111425m From than on the applied face pressure drops
forwards the middle of the drive. From the middle of the drive the applied face pressure increases
68
==PAGE:81==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
forwards the break-in of FB.
The reason for the discrepancy between the calculated support pressure and the applied face
pressure could be the fact that non of the four discussed calculation methods are capable of handling
the surcharge from the superstructures. It is diﬃcult to conclude that superstructure the size of
Frederiksberg Public Bath, next to AMH, and Frederiksberg Shopping Center would not contribute
to the face stability issue. Thus, if a proper face support pressure calculation should be preformed
for a unstable soil in a highly urbanized area the surcharge of the superstructures must be included
in the estimate.
The dramatic drop in applied face pressure which can be seen at around chainage 111950m
shows the eﬀect of the constructed break-in box at FB.

of handling the instantaneous loss of face pressure in the break-in situation by a break-in box when

plied face pressure is applied the model and the displacements obtained when the calculated support
pressure is applied the model are all whitin the same order of magnitude. This fact emphasise the
need for including, not only the adjacent structures, but more importantly the superstructures in
the applied 3D model. This is also supported by the discrepancy between the calculated support
pressure and the actual applied face pressure presented on Figure 6.4.

Rådhuspladsen is intended to serve as an actual stop on the Cityringen, serving the city center of

76
==PAGE:89==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
The tunnel stretch shown on Figure 7.2 runs from Gammel Strand Station at chainage 101361m to
Rådhuspladsen Station at chainage 100702m
The ground level at the RHP site is situated at approx. +7m. From +7m and down to +3m
the soil consists of a ﬁll layer (FY) which do not hold any particular strength. From +7m and
downward to -6m a layer of melt water sand and gravel (DS/DG) is present. At -6m and down
to -12m the soil consists of mixture of clay till and sandy clay (ML/MS). From -12m to -15m
an other layer of DS/DG is present. At -15m and down to -17m a layer of High Permeable
Upper Copenhagen Limestone (UCL(HP)) exists and ﬁnally, from -17m and downwards the soil
formation consists of Upper Copenhagen Limestone (UCL). The ground water level varies from
approx. +1.6m and down to -2m. The base of the tunnel at break-in is situated at approx. level -
16m. The tunnel face at break-in is excavated in a mixture of approx. 60% DS/DG and 40% ML/MS.
Two major challenges are present in relation to the break-in at the RHP shaft, but also in
relation to the excavation of the tunnel at a distance of approx. 100m from the shaft and into
the break-in zone. The ﬁrst challenge is the tunnel excavation in a highly urbanized historically
valuable area. The second challenge is tunnelling in very loose and soft soil.
When considering Figure 7.2 it is apparent that a dramatic change in the consistency of the
soil is present a approx. chainage 100800m. A layer of loose DS/DG suddenly appears in between
the UCL(HP) layer and ML/MS layer. Figure 7.3 presents the grain size distribution of six diﬀerent
soil samples collected in the area close to the RHP site. These soil samples are incorporated in
Figure 2.4, previously presented in Section 2.3.

Figure 7.3: Grain distribution of DG/DS at Rådhuspladsen.
Considering Figure 7.3 it appears that the grain distribution of the DS/DG is predominately
distributed in the area just before the distribution area not suitable of EPB tunnelling. The given
grain size distribution of the DS/DG layer in combination with the lack of cohesion in the DS/DG
layer indicate a potential risk of over-excavation at the break-in area of the RHP shaft.

dimensions and the generated mesh utilized in the 2D and 3D analysis. The result ﬁles of the
PLAXISTM 2D and 3D analysis are available on the attached USB-pen.

Unlike the two previous cases, the tunnel at the break-in area of RHP, is not excavated in, or
partly in, limestone but in loose sand and clay. However, the method of Leca and Dormieux is still
challenged by the soil conditions. The high value of cohesion associated with the ML/MS and the
high value of friction angles of both ML/MS as well as DG/DS, yield to an extremely high and
unrealistic compression strength. The unrealistic compression strength yield a negative value of
support pressure in the collapse case. In relation to the blow-out case. The large value of the H/D
ratio lead to Ny and Ns coeﬃcients approaching inﬁnity and leading to a meaningless result. Again,
this is valid for the upper bound and lower bound solutions of the Leca and Dormieux method and
is valid for both load cases.
The fact that the soil consist of both ML/MS and DG/DS is also a challenge to both the Jancescz
and Steiner method and the Broere method. The two methods can easily handle the soft soil of
DG/DS but can not handle the large cohesion related to the ML/MS.

As in the previous examples it is only the Anognostou and Kovàri approach that provides meaningful
results. Again,the piezometric head hF in the working chamber must be assumed equal to the
ground water table, and the cohesion of ML/MS must be neglected.
The calculated support face pressure is presented on Figure 7.6 along with the hydrostatic pressure.
Considering Figure 7.6 it is apparent that the calculated face pressure of the limestone formation
is directly related to the hydrostatic pressure. This is seen at chainage 101361m and onwards to
around chainage 100800m. The abrupt change in curvature at chainage 100800 is due to lack of
information in regard to how the actual transformation from UCL(HP) to DS/DG proceeds, see
Figure 7.2. However, the fact that soil changes from competent limestone into a soft granular soil
will also require an increase in support pressure.

face stability.
7.6
Disregarding the ML/MS layer
When considering Figure 7.2 at the break-in area, it is apparent that the ML/MS layer, holding
a large value of cohesion, is situated between two layers of DS/DG holding no cohesion at all.
Considering this fact it would be fair to assume that the ML/MS do not contribute to the stability
of the soil, in relation to face stability, in a decisive manner.

8
Conclusion
In line with the global trend of growing urban communities the demand for infrastructural solutions
have increased dramatically within the last decades and is expected to grow even more dramatically
in the near future. When cities grow in size the available space on the ground become a scarce
resource and the construction of infrastructure on ground level becomes a major challenge. This fact
has resulted in an equally increase in the demand for underground construction and the technology
that can support such a demand.
A method of underground tunnelling, that is utilized today on a wide scale, is the shielded
mechanised tunnelling method. Deploying a shielded Tunnel Boring Machine several advantages
are gained. A secure work environment, a secure and safe tunnelling is ensured as instability of the
surrounding soil formation can be kept on a minimum, limited disturbances on the surroundings
and the environment, etc.
Even though the mechanized tunnelling approach represent a far more stable and secure way
of tunnelling, compared to other tunnelling methods, challenges still exist in relation to soil stability.
One major challenge is the subject of face support. Four diﬀerent approaches to calculating the
suﬃcient face support pressure has been examined and compared to real life situations.
The method of Leca and Dormieux is a limit analysis approach to a Mohr-Coulomb material.
The method suggests an upper bound solution and a lower bound solution both including two
collapse mechanism and one blow-out mechanism. However, two major limitations are associated
with this theory. One is the ratio between the overburden and the tunnel diameter. If this ratio
exceeds a number of two the results of this approach become unclear. The other challenge is the
friction angle of the soil. Friction angles beyond 30◦are problematic to handle with this approach.
A general conclusion on the Leca and Dormieux method is that its rang of application is limited to
a shallow tunnel situation due to the inﬂuence of overburden to diameter ratio.
The method of Jancsecz and Steiner is based on a three dimension limit equilibrium model
and is mainly addressed to non cohesive granular soils. The challenges of this method is the soil
cohesion. If the cohesion exceeds 10kPa the applied limit equilibrium model will not deliver reliable
results.
The perhaps most widely adopted approach to the calculation of a suﬃcient face pressure is
the method of Anagnostou and Kovàri. This method is based on a three-dimensional static system
known as the silo theory. According to this approach the stabilizing face pressure, at limited
equilibrium, depends on the tunnel diameter, the overburden, the piezometric head in the working
chamber, the water table and on the soil properties. Assuming the piezometric head to be equal to
the hydrostatic pressure and disregarding the soil cohesion the method of Anagnostou and Kovàri
will deliver reliable predictions in wide range of soil formations. Like the previous two cases the soil
cohesion is challenging to the method of Anagnostou and Kovàri. Again, if the cohesion exceeds
10kPa the limit of the Anagnostou and Kovàri approached is reached.
Finally, the approach of Broere has been investigated.
Broere produced a method for calcu-
lating the suﬃcient pressure based on the work of Jancsecz and Steiner. However, the method of
Broere is speciﬁcally aimed at handling the face pressure in a soil formation consistent of multiple
soil layer. When utilizing the Broere approach in order to calculate the suﬃcient face support
pressure for a EPB machine, the result will not diﬀer from the result gained by the Jancsecz and
Steiner approach. Thus, the method of Broere suﬀer the same limitations as the method of Jancsecz
and Steiner.
Two major challenges exist as a common feature of all the four mentioned calculation approaches.
They can not handle large values of cohesion and they do not include the surcharge of the super-
structures in their approach. The nature of the Anagnostou and Kovàri approach make it possible
to disregard the contribution from the cohesion and yet gain reliable results. This is not possible in
89
==PAGE:102==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
the case of the Jancsecz and Steiner or the Broere method. Theoretically, the method of Leca and
Dormieux would be able to handle a large value of cohesion. But if that cohesion originates from a
clay till holding an equally large friction angle the end result would still be unclear.
The second major challenge is the issue of the surcharge. According to the Leca and Dormieuxon
method the inﬂuence of the surcharge decreases and becomes negligible as the ration between the
overburden and the tunnel diameter increases. The method of Anagnostou and Kovàri do not even
include the aspect of surcharge in the theory. In relation to Jancsecz and Steiner or the Broere
method, the surcharge can be included in the applied vertical stresses and thereby contribute to
the derivation of the suﬃcient face support pressure.
The SBV analysis indicates that the actual TBM applied face support pressure is governed
or related to the hydrostatic pressure, when excavating in a permeable competent soil formation
such as the Copenhagen Limestone. This is supported by the FB case as well. It was not possible to
verify the eﬀect of the establishment of an grout-plug in the break-in area in relation to settlement
control. However, it was indicated that the instalment of a grout-plug decreases the permeability of
the soil and thus, add to the soil stability. The surcharge does not appear to inﬂuence the face
stability when excavation is performed in competent Copenhagen Limestone.
The installation of a break-in box has proven valuable in the FB case. In a mixed soil break-in
area it is crucial to avoid an immediate loss of support pressure, especially if the break-in area
is inﬂuenced by large superstructures or adjacent buildings. The FB case emphasise the need
of including the load of the superstructures into the calculations of the suﬃcient face support pressure
Utilizing the knowledge obtained by the SBV and FB analysis in relation to securing face stability
and soil stability at the RHP beak-in situation, several points come to mind. The break-in area of
RHP consist entirely of soft soil. In such a case establishing a break-in box would be an obvious
solution in order to secure face stability and prevent serious consequences of an instantaneous loss
of support pressure.
Similar to the FB case the RHP is surrounded by adjacent buildings and the large superstructures
are present above the tunnel stretch. Based on the experience of the FB case it is crucial to include
the load of the superstructures when the suﬃcient face support pressure is estimated. This fact
should be considered, not only in the break-in area, but already from the start of the mixed soil
excavation phase starting approx. 100m before the break-in area.
From the 2D and 3D analysis of the SBV case and the 2D analysis of the RHP case it is not possible
to detect any actual eﬀect of the grout-plug in relation to reduction of settlements. However, it is a
fair assumption that a grouting campaign will decrease the permeability of the soil and reduce the
sliding eﬀect of the granular DS/DG. A grouting campaign would also reduce the risk of cavities in
the DS/DG layer beneath the ML/MS layer emerged due to local over-excavation.
A particular challenge of stabilizing the DS/DG layer by grouting is the fact that the mixed
soil phase starts approx. 100m before the break-in area. Additionally, the ground surface, above
the unstable soil, is highly developed representing major challenge in order to facilitating the actual
grouting campaign.
A major vulnerability of this project, and what prevent it conclusions to be clearer, is the 3D
numerical analysis part. It is a common feature of all three cases that the 3D simulations did
not produce reliable results. E.g. it is counter-intuitive that the 3D calculations produces the
largest settlements in the competent limestone case of SBV. Likewise, it is problematic that the 3D
calculations can not be executed successful in upper bound situation of the RHP case.
The overall problem regarding the 3D numerical analysis is the construction of the model, which is
over-simplistic. If a proper and comprehensive 3D numerical analysis should be carried out the
adjacent structures should be included in the utilized model. It can be assumed that the presence
90
==PAGE:103==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.
of the station shaft will contribute limit the size of the displacements and their distribution. In
contrast, an adjacent building and especially a superstructure will contribute in order to magnify
the displacements.
Additionally, a proper 3D model must be able to handle the inﬂuence of the permeability and the
grain size distribution. This is especially important in relation to analysis of the suﬃcient face
support pressure in soft granular soil and in relation to the stability of such soils.
Unfortunately, several questions remain unanswered in this project, mainly due to the constrains of
the applied 3D model. It was not possible to present a clear conclusion regarding the the grain size
distribution of the DS/DG and it inﬂuence in regard to the risk of over-excavation. Additionally,
no conclusion can be made on the inﬂuence of the groundwater ﬂow in the granular soil layers. An
other issue of interest is the possibility of overestimation of the friction angle of the DS/DG. The
DS/DG holds a friction angle of 38◦to 50◦. This may be a overestimation and a reduction of the
friction angle is likely to enforce the risk of over-excavation in the mixed soil phase.
91
==PAGE:104==
Face Pressure and Settlement Control of TBM Excavated Tunnels.

Note:
If necessary, support pressure and other values will be fine tuned in
the PAT in accordance with data from previous stretches.
* In the break out zone, the support pressure will raise gradually
from 0bar to the foreseen 2.2 bar. In the break in area, the support
pressure will be reduced adequately till null pressure before the
TBM reaches SKP retaining wall. TBM face pressure operating
system has to be set up in order to be able to manage promptly and
successfully unexpected increase of groundwater pressure.
